          <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">
           ×
          </span>
          10
          <sup>
           −4
          </sup>
          Earths
         </span>
         )
         <br/>
         (12.2% of Pluto)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">

          <sup>

         </span>

         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>

           <i>
            (H)
           </i>

         </div>

        <td>

         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jewitt1000km_10-0">

        </td>

       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">
          <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">
           Angular diameter
          </a>
         </div>
        </th>
        <td>
         55 milli-arcsec
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
          <a href="#cite_note-11">
           [9]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Charon
       </b>
       , also known as
       <b>
        (134340) Pluto I
       </b>
       , is the largest of the
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Pluto" title="Moons of Pluto">
        five known natural satellites
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">
        dwarf planet
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
        Pluto
       </a>
       . It was discovered in 1978 at the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory" title="United States Naval Observatory">
        United States Naval Observatory
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">
        Washington, D.C.
       </a>
       , using photographic plates taken at the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory_Flagstaff_Station" title="United States Naval Observatory Flagstaff Station">
        United States Naval Observatory Flagstaff Station
       </a>
       (NOFS). With half the diameter and one eighth the mass of Pluto, it is a very large moon in comparison to its parent body. Its gravitational influence is such that the
       <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">
        barycenter
       </a>
       of the Pluto–Charon system lies outside Pluto. In September 2016, astronomers announced that the reddish-brown cap of the north pole of Charon is composed of
       <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">
        tholins
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">
        organic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">
        macromolecules
       </a>
       that may be essential ingredients of
       <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">
        life
       </a>
       , and produced from
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        methane
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
        nitrogen
       </a>
       and related gases released from the
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Pluto" title="Atmosphere of Pluto">
        atmosphere of Pluto
       </a>
       and transferred over about 19,000 km (12,000 mi) distance to the orbiting moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160914_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160914-12">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">
         New Horizons
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft is the only probe that has visited the Pluto system. It approached Charon to within 27,000 km (17,000 mi) in 2015.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">
        Discovery
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charon_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Discovery">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charon_Discovery.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1072" data-file-width="1416" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Charon_Discovery.jpg/220px-Charon_Discovery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Charon_Discovery.jpg/330px-Charon_Discovery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Charon_Discovery.jpg/440px-Charon_Discovery.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charon_Discovery.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Charon's discovery at the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_Observatory" title="Naval Observatory">
          Naval Observatory
         </a>
         Flagstaff Station as a time-varying bulge on the image of Pluto (seen near the top at left, but absent on the right). Negative image.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NH-Charon-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1800" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/NH-Charon-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg/220px-NH-Charon-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/NH-Charon-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg/330px-NH-Charon-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/NH-Charon-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg/440px-NH-Charon-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NH-Charon-Day1-TenImages-20150714-20151120.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Mosaic of best-resolution images of Charon from different angles
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           needs additional citations for
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            verification
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please help
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Charon_(moon)&amp;action=edit">
            improve this article
           </a>
           by
           <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">
            adding citations to reliable sources
           </a>
           . Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (June 2017)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Charon was discovered by United States Naval Observatory astronomer
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Christy" title="James Christy">
        James Christy
       </a>
       , using the 1.55-meter (61 in) telescope at
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory_Flagstaff_Station" title="United States Naval Observatory Flagstaff Station">
        United States Naval Observatory Flagstaff Station
       </a>
       (NOFS),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On June 22, 1978, he had been examining highly magnified images of
       <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
        Pluto
       </a>
       on
       <a href="/wiki/Photographic_plate" title="Photographic plate">
        photographic plates
       </a>
       taken with the telescope two months prior. Christy noticed that a slight elongation appeared periodically. The bulge was confirmed on plates dating back to April 29, 1965.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
        International Astronomical Union
       </a>
       formally announced to the world on July 7, 1978.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC3241_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC3241-15">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Subsequent observations of Pluto determined that the bulge was due to a smaller accompanying body. The periodicity of the bulge corresponded to Pluto's rotation period, which was previously known from Pluto's
       <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">
        light curve
       </a>
       . This indicated a
       <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_orbit" title="Synchronous orbit">
        synchronous orbit
       </a>
       , which strongly suggested that the bulge effect was real and not spurious. This resulted in reassessments of Pluto's size, mass, and other physical characteristics, because the calculated mass and
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedo
       </a>
       of the Pluto–Charon system had previously been attributed to Pluto alone.
      </p>
      <p>
       Doubts about Charon's existence were erased when it and Pluto entered a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_eclipses_on_Pluto" title="Solar eclipses on Pluto">
        five-year period of mutual eclipses and transits
       </a>
       between 1985 and 1990. This occurs when the Pluto–Charon
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_plane_(astronomy)" title="Orbital plane (astronomy)">
        orbital plane
       </a>
       is edge-on as seen from Earth, which only happens at two intervals in Pluto's 248-year orbital period. It was fortuitous that one of these intervals happened to occur soon after Charon's discovery.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Name">
        Name
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charon_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Name">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Charon was originally known by the temporary designation
       <b>
        S/1978 P 1
       </b>
       , according to the then recently instituted convention. On June 24, 1978, Christy first suggested the name
       <i>
        Charon
       </i>
       as a scientific-sounding version of his wife Charlene's nickname, "Char".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shilling_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Shilling-16">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UnivToday_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-UnivToday-17">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although colleagues at the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory" title="United States Naval Observatory">
        Naval Observatory
       </a>
       proposed
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Persephone" title="Persephone">
         Persephone
        </a>
       </i>
       , Christy stuck with
       <i>
        Charon
       </i>
       after discovering that it coincidentally refers to a Greek mythological figure:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shilling_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Shilling-16">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Charon_(mythology)" title="Charon (mythology)">
        Charon
       </a>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ɛər/: 'are' in 'bare'">
            ɛər
           </span>
           <span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">
            ɒ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       or
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ɛər/: 'are' in 'bare'">
            ɛər
           </span>
           <span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">
            ən
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
        Greek
       </a>
       Χάρων) is the ferryman of the dead, closely associated in myth with the god
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hades_(mythology)" title="Hades (mythology)">
        Hades
       </a>
       , whom the Romans identified with their god
       <a href="/wiki/Pluto_(mythology)" title="Pluto (mythology)">
        Pluto
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
        IAU
       </a>
       officially adopted the name in late 1985 and it was announced on January 3, 1986.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is minor debate over the preferred pronunciation of the name. The practice of following the classical pronunciation established for the mythological ferryman Charon (IPA
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈkɛ:rən]
       </span>
       ) is used by major English-language dictionaries, such as the Merriam-Webster and Oxford English Dictionary.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These indicate only one pronunciation of "Charon" when referring specifically to Pluto's moon: with an initial "k" sound. Speakers of many languages other than English, and many English-speaking astronomers as well, follow this pronunciation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9P_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-9P-21">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       However, Christy himself pronounced the
       <i>
        ch
       </i>
       as
       <i>
        sh
       </i>
       (IPA
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃ]
       </span>
       ), after his wife Charlene. Because of this, as an acknowledgement of Christy and sometimes as an
       <a href="/wiki/In-joke" title="In-joke">
        in-joke
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Shibboleth" title="Shibboleth">
        shibboleth
       </a>
       , the initial
       <i>
        sh
       </i>
       pronunciation is common among astronomers when speaking English,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [note 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9P_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-9P-21">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and this is the prescribed pronunciation at NASA and of the
       <i>
        New Horizons
       </i>
       team.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [note 4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">
        Formation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charon_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Formation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Simulation work published in 2005 by Robin Canup suggested that Charon could have been formed by a collision around 4.5
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" title="1000000000 (number)">
        billion
       </a>
       years ago, much like
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       . In this model, a large
       <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
        Kuiper belt
       </a>
       object struck Pluto at high velocity, destroying itself and blasting off much of Pluto's outer
       <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">
        mantle
       </a>
       , and Charon coalesced from the debris.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, such an impact should result in an icier Charon and rockier Pluto than scientists have found. It is now thought that Pluto and Charon might have been two bodies that collided before going into orbit about each other. The collision would have been violent enough to boil off volatile ices like
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        methane
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        CH
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         4
        </span>
       </span>
       ) but not violent enough to have destroyed either body. The very similar density of Pluto and Charon implies that the parent bodies were not fully differentiated when the impact occurred.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_2015_6-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stern_2015-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit">
        Orbit
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charon_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Orbit">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="322" data-file-width="640" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif/220px-Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif/330px-Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif/440px-Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pluto-Charon_system-new.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A view of the Pluto–Charon system showing that Pluto orbits a point outside itself. Also visible is the mutual
         <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">
          tidal locking
         </a>
         between the two bodies.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Charon and Pluto orbit each other every 6.387 days. The two objects are
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">
        gravitationally locked
       </a>
       to one another, so each keeps the same face towards the other. This is a case of mutual tidal locking, as compared to that of the Earth and the Moon, where the Moon always shows the same face to Earth, but not vice versa. The average distance between Charon and Pluto is 19,570 kilometres (12,160 mi). The discovery of Charon allowed astronomers to calculate accurately the mass of the Plutonian system, and mutual
       <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">
        occultations
       </a>
       revealed their sizes. However, neither indicated the two bodies' individual masses, which could only be estimated, until the discovery of Pluto's outer moons in late 2005. Details in the orbits of the outer moons revealed that Charon has approximately 12% of the mass of Pluto.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie_2006_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Buie_2006-5">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">
        Physical characteristics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charon_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Charon" title="Geology of Charon">
        Geology of Charon
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_geological_features_on_Charon" title="List of geological features on Charon">
        List of geological features on Charon
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charon,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2026" data-file-width="3000" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Charon%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/220px-Charon%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Charon%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/330px-Charon%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Charon%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/440px-Charon%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charon,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Size comparisons:
         <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
          Earth
         </a>
         , the
         <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
          Moon
         </a>
         , and Charon
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Charon's diameter is 1,212 kilometres (753 mi), just over half that of Pluto,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_2015_6-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stern_2015-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_2017_7-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stern_2017-7">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and larger than the dwarf planet
       <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">
        Ceres
       </a>
       , and the twelfth largest
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
        natural satellite
       </a>
       in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">
        solar system
       </a>
       . Charon is sufficiently massive to have
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">
        collapsed
       </a>
       into a spheroid under its own gravity. Charon's slow rotation means that there is almost no flattening. Its equatorial and polar radii differ by less than 1%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_2015_6-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stern_2015-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Interior">
        Interior
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charon_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Interior">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charonsinre.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="340" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Charonsinre.jpg/220px-Charonsinre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Charonsinre.jpg/330px-Charonsinre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/Charonsinre.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charonsinre.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The two conflicting theories about Charon's internal structure
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Charon's volume and mass allow calculation of its density,
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7003170200000000000♠
        </span>
        1.702
        <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ±
        </span>
        0.017 g/cm
        <sup>
         3
        </sup>
       </span>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_2017_7-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stern_2017-7">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       from which it can be determined that Charon is slightly less dense than Pluto and suggesting a composition of 55% rock to 45% ice (± 5%), whereas Pluto is about 70% rock. The difference is considerably lower than that of most suspected collisional satellites. Before
       <i>
        New Horizons'
       </i>
       flyby, there were two conflicting theories about Charon's internal structure: some scientists thought Charon to be a differentiated body like Pluto, with a rocky core and an icy mantle, whereas others thought it would be uniform throughout.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Evidence in support of the former position was found in 2007, when observations by the Gemini Observatory of patches of ammonia hydrates and water crystals on the surface of Charon suggested the presence of active cryogeysers. The fact that the ice was still in crystalline form suggested it had been deposited recently, because solar radiation would have degraded it to an
       <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_ice" title="Amorphous ice">
        amorphous
       </a>
       state after roughly thirty thousand years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ice_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ice-29">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Surface">
        Surface
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charon_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Surface">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charon-Neutral-Bright-Release.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1682" data-file-width="1682" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Charon-Neutral-Bright-Release.jpg/170px-Charon-Neutral-Bright-Release.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Charon-Neutral-Bright-Release.jpg/255px-Charon-Neutral-Bright-Release.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Charon-Neutral-Bright-Release.jpg/340px-Charon-Neutral-Bright-Release.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charon-Neutral-Bright-Release.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Charon in enhanced color to bring out differences in surface composition.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Unlike Pluto's surface, which is composed of
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
        nitrogen
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        methane
       </a>
       ices, Charon's surface appears to be dominated by the less
       <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">
        volatile
       </a>
       water ice. In 2007, observations by the
       <a href="/wiki/Gemini_Observatory" title="Gemini Observatory">
        Gemini Observatory
       </a>
       of patches of ammonia hydrates and water crystals on the surface of Charon suggested the presence of active
       <a href='/wiki/Geyser#"Geysers"_elsewhere_in_the_Solar_System' title="Geyser">
        cryogeysers
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">
        cryovolcanoes
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ice_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ice-29">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Photometric mapping of Charon's surface shows a latitudinal trend in
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedo
       </a>
       , with a bright equatorial band and darker poles. The north polar region is dominated by a very large dark area informally dubbed "
       <a href="/wiki/Mordor_Macula" title="Mordor Macula">
        Mordor
       </a>
       " by the
       <i>
        New Horizons
       </i>
       team.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBC1_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NBC1-32">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The favored explanation for this phenomenon is that they are formed by condensation of gases that escaped from
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Pluto" title="Atmosphere of Pluto">
        Pluto's atmosphere
       </a>
       . In winter, the temperature is −258 °C, and these gases, which include nitrogen, carbon monoxide, and methane, condense into their solid forms; when these ices are subjected to solar radiation, they chemically react to form various reddish
       <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">
        tholins
       </a>
       . Later, when the area is again heated by the Sun as Charon's seasons change, the temperature at the pole rises to −213 °C, resulting in the volatiles sublimating and escaping Charon, leaving only the tholins behind. Over millions of years, the residual tholin builds up thick layers, obscuring the icy crust.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition to Mordor,
       <i>
        New Horizons
       </i>
       found evidence of extensive past geology that suggests that Charon is probably differentiated;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBC1_32-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NBC1-32">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in particular, the southern hemisphere has fewer craters than the northern and is considerably less rugged, suggesting that a massive resurfacing event—perhaps prompted by the partial or complete freezing of an internal ocean—occurred at some point in the past and removed many of the earlier craters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Charon has a series of extensive grabens or canyons, such as
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_geological_features_on_Charon" title="List of geological features on Charon">
        Serenity Chasma
       </a>
       , which extend as an equatorial belt for at least 1,000 km. Argo Chasma potentially reaches as deep as 9 km, with steep cliffs that may rival
       <a href="/wiki/Verona_Rupes" title="Verona Rupes">
        Verona Rupes
       </a>
       on
       <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">
        Miranda
       </a>
       for the title of tallest cliff in the solar system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA20036-Charon-YoungestCrater-20150714.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1266" data-file-width="2416" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/PIA20036-Charon-YoungestCrater-20150714.jpg/220px-PIA20036-Charon-YoungestCrater-20150714.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/PIA20036-Charon-YoungestCrater-20150714.jpg/330px-PIA20036-Charon-YoungestCrater-20150714.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/PIA20036-Charon-YoungestCrater-20150714.jpg/440px-PIA20036-Charon-YoungestCrater-20150714.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA20036-Charon-YoungestCrater-20150714.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The youngest crater of Charon.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mountain_in_a_moat">
        Mountain in a moat
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charon_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mountain in a moat">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In a released photo by
       <i>
        New Horizons
       </i>
       , an unusual surface feature has captivated and baffled the scientist team of the mission. The image reveals a mountain rising out of a depression, in a zoomed in view. It's "a large mountain sitting in a moat", said Jeff Moore, of NASA’s
       <a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">
        Ames Research Center
       </a>
       , in a statement. "This is a feature that has geologists stunned and stumped", he added.
       <i>
        New Horizons
       </i>
       captured the photo from a distance of 49,000 miles (79,000 km).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_and_exploration">
        Observation and exploration
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charon_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Observation and exploration">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Since the first blurred images of the moon
       <small>
        (1)
       </small>
       , images showing Pluto and Charon resolved into separate disks were taken for the first time by the
       <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
        Hubble Space Telescope
       </a>
       in the 1990s
       <small>
        (2)
       </small>
       . The telescope was responsible for the best, yet low quality images of the moon. In 1994, the clearest picture of the Pluto-Charon system showed two distinct and well defined circles
       <small>
        (3)
       </small>
       . The image was taken by Hubble's
       <a href="/wiki/Faint_Object_Camera" title="Faint Object Camera">
        Faint Object Camera
       </a>
       (FOC) when the system was 2.6 billion miles (4.4 billion kilometers) away from Earth
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Later, the development of
       <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_optics" title="Adaptive optics">
        adaptive optics
       </a>
       made it possible to resolve Pluto and Charon into separate disks using ground-based telescopes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UnivToday_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-UnivToday-17">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In June 2015, the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">
         New Horizons
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft captured consecutive images of the Pluto–Charon system as it approached it. The images were put together in an animation. It was the best image of Charon to that date
       <small>
        (4)
       </small>
       . In July 2015, the
       <i>
        New Horizons
       </i>
       spacecraft made its closest approach to the Pluto system. It is the only spacecraft to date to have visited and studied Charon.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tnone center">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
        Classification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charon_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre_of_mass#Barycenter" title="Centre of mass">
        center of mass
       </a>
       (barycenter) of the Pluto–Charon system lies outside either body. Because neither object truly orbits the other, and Charon has 12.2% the mass of Pluto, it has been argued that Charon should be considered to be part of a
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_system" title="Binary system">
        binary system
       </a>
       with Pluto. The
       <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
        International Astronomical Union
       </a>
       (IAU) states that Charon is considered to be just a satellite of Pluto, but the idea that Charon might be classified a dwarf planet in its own right may be considered at a later date.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU_DP_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAU_DP-40">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In a draft proposal for the
       <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">
        2006 redefinition
       </a>
       of the term, the IAU proposed that a planet be defined as a body that orbits the Sun that is large enough for gravitational forces to render the object (nearly) spherical. Under this proposal, Charon would have been classified as a planet, because the draft explicitly defined a planetary satellite as one in which the barycenter lies within the major body. In the final definition, Pluto was reclassified as a
       <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">
        dwarf planet
       </a>
       , but the formal definition of a planetary satellite was not decided upon. Charon is not in the list of dwarf planets currently recognized by the IAU.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU_DP_40-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAU_DP-40">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Had the draft proposal been accepted, even the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       would be classified as a planet in billions of years when the
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">
        tidal acceleration
       </a>
       that is gradually moving the Moon away from Earth takes it far enough away that the center of mass of the system no longer lies within Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moonplanet_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-moonplanet-41">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The other moons of Pluto,
       <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">
        Nix
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">
        Hydra
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">
        Kerberos
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">
        Styx
       </a>
       , orbit the same barycenter, but they are not large enough to be spherical, and they are simply considered to be satellites of Pluto (or of Pluto–Charon).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NixHydra_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NixHydra-42">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>

      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-2">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-2">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The latter per the anglicized pronunciation of the
           <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
            Greek
           </a>
           :
           <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">
            Χάρων
           </span>
           .
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-calculated-4">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-calculated_4-0">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Calculated on the basis of other parameters.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-22">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-22">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Astronomer Mike Brown can be heard pronouncing it
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            [ˈʃɛɹ
            <s>
             ɪ
            </s>
            n]
           </span>
           in ordinary conversation on the KCET interview [
           <cite class="citation web">
            <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20081006182705/http://www.pluggd.tv/audio/channels/kcet_podcast__hammer_conversations/episodes/2h10l" rel="nofollow">
             "Julia Sweeney and Michael E. Brown"
            </a>
            .
            <i>
             Hammer Conversations: KCET podcast
            </i>
            . 2007. Archived from
            <a class="external text" href="http://www.pluggd.tv/audio/channels/kcet_podcast__hammer_conversations/episodes/2h10l" rel="nofollow">
             the original
            </a>
            on 2008-10-06
            <span class="reference-accessdate">
             . Retrieved
             <span class="nowrap">
              2008-10-01
             </span>
            </span>
            .
           </cite>
           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Hammer+Conversations%3A+KCET+podcast&amp;rft.atitle=Julia+Sweeney+and+Michael+E.+Brown&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.pluggd.tv%2Faudio%2Fchannels%2Fkcet_podcast__hammer_conversations%2Fepisodes%2F2h10l&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACharon+%28moon%29">
            <span style="display:none;">
            </span>
           </span>
           ] at 42min 48sec. Being a long-time resident of California, he does not distinguish the
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /ær/
           </span>
           vowel of the name
           <i>
            Sharon
           </i>
           and the
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /ɛər/
           </span>
           vowel of the classical pronunciation of
           <i>
            Charon
           </i>
           .
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-26">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-26">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hal_Weaver&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hal Weaver (page does not exist)">
            Hal Weaver
           </a>
           , who led the team that discovered Nix and Hydra, also pronounces it
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            [ˈʃɛɹ
            <s>
             ɪ
            </s>
            n]
           </span>
           (
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /ˈʃærən/
           </span>
           with a generic American accent) on the
           <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Discovery_Science_Channel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Discovery Science Channel (page does not exist)">
            Discovery Science Channel
           </a>
           documentary
           <i>
            Passport to Pluto
           </i>
           , premiered 2006-01-15.
          </span>
